A localization accuracy evaluation was performed, yielding an overall accuracy of 91.28% in extracting circuit components. The work in [ 133], referred to as Img2Sim and published in 2022, employs an Artificial Neural Network (ANN) approach [ 134] to convert circuit schematics into a netlist. This work builds upon a prior approach published in [ 135] that used deep learning to recognize circuit components, and [ 136], which involved identifying hand-drawn circuit images using OCR. The work published in [ 137] is also referenced. However, while these methods primarily focus on passive components, Img2Sim also aims to support active components. A dataset comprising 330 circuit diagrams was used for testing, utilizing YOLOv5 for element recognition, a Hough Transform for node detection, and OCR for text recognition, with the final results generated as a netlist. Their presented method detects circuit components with an accuracy of 98%, and generates correct netlists with an accuracy of 90%. This demonstrates that, while there is room for improvement in circuit diagram extraction from images, significantly more research is needed to accurately represent these diagrams using netlist files post-extraction, in order to support the intended downstream applications. Another method, presented in 2022 [ 138], utilized a DNN to generate netlists from hand-drawn circuit diagrams, building upon the CNN-based classification approach published in [ 137] and other works such as [ 136, 139, 140, 141]. YOLOv5 was utilized for component recognition, adaptive thresholding and bounding box extraction for terminal recognition, as well as k-means clustering for detecting intersections. The Hough Transform was applied for line detection and node identification. This approach was implemented using PyTorch and subsequently evaluated using a dataset of 154 hand-drawn circuits (augmented to 388 training images). It achieved a mAP score with 0.5 IoU of 98.2% for component detection, 92% accuracy for node recognition, and 80% overall circuit reconstruction accuracy. Their results indicated good extraction performance for simple circuit diagrams using passive components; however, further research was needed to improve the accuracy of more complex diagrams involving active components and integrated circuit components. Additionally, these methods required further evaluation using larger datasets spanning a wider range of circuit diagram representations. The authors of [ 142], published in 2021, developed a method that leverages augmented reality techniques to convert hand-drawn circuit diagrams into other representations using a smartphone. The author's work expands upon [ 143, 144], which used an SVM approach for circuit recognition. Feature extraction is performed using a custom algorithm that combines region proposal using SURF, Eigen, and FAST. Dilation and Hough Transform are also incorporated as part of the processing workflow. A capsule network architecture (CapsNet) [ 145] is used instead of a CNN due to limitations with CNNs, as discussed by the authors in their paper, primarily related to the significantly higher training dataset size required for CNNs compared to CapsNets. The final step in their approach is circuit simulation, which determines whether the extraction produces a viable netlist, concluding with the export of the netlist generated by the previous steps. With a custom dataset comprising 800 circuit diagrams used for evaluation, and utilizing the mAP scoring metric with an IoU threshold of 0.3, the resulting score for this approach is 93.64%. Circuit diagram extraction has attracted increasing attention in recent years, driven by advancements in machine learning that enable more accurate recognition and interpretation of complex schematics, as shown in Figure 10. Using Litmaps, this analysis illustrates the relationships and interdependencies among the reviewed papers. The authors of [ 146, 147, 148] each developed their own circuit extraction techniques that build upon the work in [ 141] by expanding its scope, which previously focused exclusively on text extraction from hand-drawn diagrams. The techniques discussed in [ 146, 149] also reference the research from [ 139], which focuses on recognizing hand-drawn schematics. Published in 2022, the work in [ 146] presents a model that can analyze hand-drawn or computer-generated schematics, specifically of power converter circuits, generate netlists from them, and simulate them in real-time. It is inspired by older works, such as [ 133, 135, 138, 139, 140, 141, 142, 150]. For evaluating their presented technique, they developed their own dataset. The images underwent noise filtering to help build a more robust system. A variant of YOLO called You Only Learn Once Representation (YOLOR) [ 151] was used to detect components. Hough Transform was used to detect lines. Edge detection is performed to improve the performance of the HT. Node recognition was conducted using a k-means clustering technique [ 152]. The resulting netlist is then evaluated and simulated using PySpice. The technique was trained on a dataset of 176 hand-drawn diagrams, achieving a mAP0.5 score was 91.6%. This work's biggest shortcoming is in diagrams involving surface-mount components in Printed Circuit Board (PCB) schematics. The authors also compared their performance obtained using PySpice with performance metrics from LTSpice, which confirmed the accuracy of the generated netlists for diagrams where extraction was conducted successfully. Figure 10. Citation graph visualization for circuit diagrams [ 129, 133, 136, 139, 140, 142, 146, 148, 149, 150, 153, 154, 155, 156, 157, 158, 159, 160]. Figure 10. Citation graph visualization for circuit diagrams [ 129, 133, 136, 139, 140, 142, 146, 148, 149, 150, 153, 154, 155, 156, 157, 158, 159, 160]. In 2023, Bayer et al. published [ 148], which aimed to facilitate extracting component labels and junctions from circuit diagram extraction using keypoint extraction and instance segmentation. For evaluation, they extended the Circuit Graph Hand-Drawn (CGHD) dataset, which contains 2208 hand-drawn circuit diagrams, by creating 245 binary segmentation maps with 18,276 polygon annotations. Additionally, their approach is inspired by [ 136, 138, 141]. The presented approach first separates the background from objects using binarization maps, which are then further refined using the LabelME annotation tool to clean up the coarse polygon masks. The image was then simplified by layering coarse polygons with binarization maps. Contour detection was employed to enhance the precision of the polygons, reducing ambiguity in the annotations. Keypoint Generation precisely identifies connection points on electrical symbols after applying the morphological operator erosion to reduce the impact of noise. A technique called Keypoint Port Assignment is then performed to link detected connection points on electrical symbols to their correct terminals, with the final step being the creation of electrical circuit graph representations. A Mask R-CNN model trained on this extended dataset achieved a maximum training mask accuracy of 94%. This demonstrates that instance segmentation approaches can yield commendable results for circuit diagram analysis. Closely related to the work in [ 146], the authors of [ 147] in 2023 published their technique that combines OCR, structured pattern extraction with regular expressions, and shape matching using geometric methods inspired by past work published by [ 136, 141, 148]. The aforementioned CGHD dataset, containing 2304 images, was used for their testing. During the text detection stage, Faster R-CNN was used. For handwritten recognition, the approach relies on a combination of CNN and LSTM, as well as Regular expressions (RegEx) for text label identification. The Euclidean distance was the metric employed by the authors for text symbol assignment, and prefix resolution was used for unit of measurement resolution. The work demonstrated promising results. It excels at resistors, capacitors, and voltage labels. However, subscript annotations and negation lines are not yet supported, and additional work is needed to address these and related capabilities. More work is also needed for the normalization of electrical values. Lastly, a consistency evaluation must be conducted to confirm the integrity of the graph's generated output representation. This 2024 research paper [ 153] proposed another technique for circuit diagram extraction, influenced by earlier techniques such as [ 161], which was created to help to recognize faulty analog circuits using neural networks, as well as [ 138, 140, 154]. Following the initial image acquisition, they first convert the image into a grayscale representation. YOLOv8 is leveraged for component recognition with high accuracy, whereas a CNN and Line Detection are used during image segmentation to classify the structure of the circuit components and their corresponding value labels. The resulting text representation of the circuit diagram is used in a subsequent analysis stage to evaluate the accuracy of current and voltage levels at each node present in the circuit. 50 diagrams were used for testing, performed on a NVIDIA RTX 4070 super GPU, and implemented using Python. This evaluation yielded an 84% success rate, with 42 out of 50 images extracted without errors. However, a more comprehensive evaluation using a significantly larger dataset is needed to properly assess this technique in comparison to others in this domain. In 2024, Hemker et al. [ 155] presented another technique that aims to convert schematics into netlists, based on discussions from [ 133, 137, 138, 140, 150]. Their approach decomposes the problem into three subtasks: component detection using YOLOv7, line detection using a Lightweight convolutional neural network (L-CNN) [ 162], and text detection using EasyOCR with a CRAFT model. The dataset consists of 164 usable pages extracted from 67 PDFs from Altium Designer schematics. For OCR training, 124 images with 46,498 text boxes were used, with the CRAFT model fine-tuned from a checkpoint pre-trained on SynthText and ICDAR 2015. The component detection achieved an mAP@0.95 of at least 73% for most component classes. Although the obtained results were promising for component and line recognition, significant improvements are still needed, particularly in detecting small text labels, such as pin numbers used on integrated circuit components, and handling varying symbol orientations. A similar effort, published in 2024 by Kelly and Cole [ 156], aimed to address the process of extracting information from digitized circuit diagram images by using a custom OCR model, improving on [ 163]. Here, Otsu's method was used for binarization and skeletonization to gain a specific contour size of 1 pixel wide for the circuit. A Progressive Probabilistic Hough Transform (PPHT) [ 164] was then employed for recognizing lines in the circuit, and Tesseract OCR was used to train a custom model to identify component symbols. This approach was then implemented as a software solution and evaluated using a custom dataset of 15 schematics, with three being hand-drawn and 12 obtained from various publishers. Their pattern-recognition algorithm correctly identified the locations of 166 out of 194 components, achieving 85.6% accuracy. The custom OCR model achieved 99.6% accuracy on synthetic training data, though performance on real schematics varied significantly depending on image resolution and component variety. In 2025, Gao et al. [ 149] presented a circuit diagram retrieval method using hierarchical graph representation. This was inspired by efforts on the automatic generation of detection rules for circuit identification published in [ 139, 165, 166], which leverage machine learning to recognize circuit components. Their approach first recognizes circuit components using GAM-YOLO [ 167] with Otsu's method for preprocessing, then converts diagrams into hierarchical graph representations for retrieval. Graph Edit Distance (GED) [ 168] was employed as the graph matching metric, while IoU and mAP were used for component detection evaluation. The method was evaluated on a dataset of 2275 circuit diagrams (2048 training, 227 testing), achieving a component detection F1-Score of 91.0%. For circuit diagram retrieval, the hierarchical approach achieved an average precision of 88.1%, significantly outperforming traditional image-based retrieval methods. The results demonstrate that graph-based representation enables effective circuit diagram retrieval and supports various downstream tasks. Additionally, a 2025 paper by Arun et al. [ 157] discussed using deep learning for circuit diagram extraction, referencing [ 129, 133, 138, 141, 150] as inspirations for their work. The study expanded the Kaggle dataset [ 169] from 12 to 25 component classes by adding operational amplifiers (OP-AMPs), BJTs, MOSFETs, JFETs, switches, and wires, totaling 4800 training images. The authors developed a custom multi-layer CNN model that achieved a training accuracy of 99% and a test accuracy of 96%, with precision and recall scores of 96.3% and 96.1%, respectively. The method significantly outperformed prior approaches shown in their comparative analysis. The authors also created a Flask-based web application that allowed users to upload hand-drawn circuit images and automatically generate LTspice-compatible netlist files (.asc format), demonstrating notable improvements in the automated extraction and interpretation of circuit diagrams. Finally, building on [ 156], the authors of [ 158] conducted an experiment that individually extracted each layer of a circuit diagram, allowing for a more accurate extraction. It aims to improve upon prior efforts such as [ 156] for better support of digitizing circuit diagrams. It also incorporates efforts from [ 170], which specifically aims to improve the detection of small objects in circuit diagrams. YOLOv7 was used for element layer detection within a circuit diagram, PaddleOCR [ 171] and PaddleClas image classification [ 172] were used for text layer extraction. The author used a connection relationship algorithm to understand the relationship between each diagram. Their framework was implemented using Python 3.9, PyTorch 1.13, and OpenCV, running on a host with Ubuntu 20.04 LTS and a single NVIDIA RTX 3090 GPU. Using a dataset containing 5000 diagrams and an Element Feature Library (EFL), this method achieved an F1-Score of 99.6% with diagrams featuring various backgrounds. This highly promising result shows the significant advances made in this research domain in recent years. The authors noted that future work would focus on handling extremely low-quality diagrams and expanding the approach to other types of industrial diagrams. 4.5.2. Electrical Circuit Diagram Review Summary Table 5 contains the 14 schematic diagram extraction papers that were discussed in this part of our survey. It is our estimation that 10 out of 14 methods are suitable for various application domains, whereas the remaining 4 papers demonstrate earlier efforts that paved the way for later significant advances in the accurate and detailed extraction of electrical circuit diagrams from images. All of these efforts, however, could benefit from further evaluation using larger benchmark datasets to better compare their individual performance across the variety of algorithms and ML techniques proposed and studied over the years for this application domain. Table 5. Table of circuit schematic extraction scoring. 4.6. Timing Diagram Analysis 4.6.1. Review of Identified Papers A category of technical illustrations that stands on its own is timing diagrams. Unlike many other diagram types, the importance of line positioning is significantly elevated, and thus it mandates highly accurate extraction to accurately represent signal change timing. A study published in 2023 developed a method named TD-Magic [ 173], designed to translate timing diagram images into formal specifications using a neuro-symbolic approach. The method uses object detection for rising and falling edge detection, OCR for text label extraction, and image processing techniques to capture synchronization patterns. Their approach was inspired by signal digitization work from the biomedical domain, particularly ECG signal extraction from PDF documents [ 174]. Feature recognition was performed using a custom Signal-Edge Detector (SED) module with YOLOv5 for edge detection, and PaddleOCR for text detection and recognition. A Line-and-Arrow-Detection (LAD) module using image processing morphological operations was employed for identifying arrows and annotation lines. Semantic Interpretation (SEI) was used to generate Strict Partial Order (SPO) representations from the extracted features. To overcome the lack of labeled training data, the authors developed L-TD-G, a synthetic timing diagram generator that produced 15,000 labeled training images. The dataset for evaluation included 30 timing diagrams (29 from industry, one manually drawn). On synthetic validation data, the edge detection achieved near-perfect precision and recall, with mAP@0.5 of 99.5%. For industrial timing diagrams, 23 out of 30 (76.7%) were successfully extracted at the structural level, with 15 (50%) achieving complete accuracy at both structural and textual levels. The results demonstrate promise for the automated extraction of formal specifications from timing diagram images. Another timing diagram extraction technique was introduced in 2025, called TD-Interpreter [ 175]. While it was inspired by TD-Magic [ 173], it differed in its approach and scope. TD-Magic focuses on extracting SPO relations as formal specifications, while TD-Interpreter leverages multimodal learning to interpret timing diagrams through a visual question-answering environment. The work was also inspired by LLaVA, a multimodal large language model. TD-Interpreter utilizes a ViT to process images and extract visual features, which are then processed by the LLaVA-based language model for reasoning and response generation. Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) was employed for efficient fine-tuning. To address the lack of labeled training data, the authors developed a synthetic data generation workflow, creating a training dataset comprising 4942 timing diagram images for caption-based tasks and 5292 images for reasoning-based tasks. The model was trained on 8 NVIDIA A800 GPUs and evaluated on a separate test set, achieving a BLEU-4 score of 95.9 and ROUGE-1 score of 96.7. The results demonstrate that fine-tuning lightweight multimodal LLMs on domain-specific synthetic data can achieve highly accurate timing diagram interpretation, significantly outperforming untuned models like GPT-4o. 4.6.2. Timing Diagram Review Summary Table 6 shows the comparison of TD-Magic and TD-Interpreter. The applicability of TD-Magic is limited by its low score achieved over a comparatively small dataset. However, it did succeed on several test cases and thus warrants further research to improve its performance, especially for use cases with limited computational resources such as in edge devices. The TD-Interpreter, on the other hand, employs a significantly more advanced VLM-driven approach that includes reasoning capabilities and was tested on a much larger dataset. Thus, among the two, TD-Interpreter is a more promising approach that, given the required computational resources, will be able to achieve significantly better results for downstream applications depending on accurate timing diagram extraction. Table 6. Table of timing diagram extraction scoring. Additionally, for this survey, we also considered and reviewed a research domain distinct from the technical diagrams that form the primary focus of this survey, but that may shed some light on related image content extraction approaches that have the potential to be applicable to some of the discussed technical diagram types: time series diagrams. Unlike timing diagrams, which specifically capture the discrete behavior of digital or analog signals for hardware verification, time series diagrams more broadly visualize quantitative data trends over time, and are commonly found in financial reports, scientific publications, and within a data analytics context. Recent work in this area [ 176, 177, 178, 179, 180, 181] explored various approaches for extracting features and detecting anomalies from time series data. These methods typically employ convolutional neural networks for spatial feature extraction, recurrent networks such as LSTMs for modeling temporal dependencies, transformer-based architectures for attention mechanisms, and graph neural networks for capturing inter-variable relationships. While these approaches demonstrated effective solutions for time series analysis and anomaly detection, they target a different application domain, only distantly related to the types of technical diagrams examined in this survey, and are therefore included here as related work outside the primary scope of understanding technical diagrams. 4.7. Comparative Observations and Summary Across Illustration Types Based on the reviewed research papers across all four technical diagram types, transformer-based approaches consistently demonstrate strong performance in terms of accuracy, adaptability, and robustness, regardless of diagram type. While these transformer-based approaches are computationally expensive, more lightweight models are available for application scenarios where efficiency outweighs accuracy, such as in edge computing or mobile applications. Object detection frameworks such as YOLO and LLMs such as the various GPT models also offer reliable performance across diverse scenarios. Traditional extraction methods remain useful for specific tasks, but combining them with modern techniques generally yields the most effective results across a wide range of situations. It is important to note that different diagram types attract varying levels of research attention, which influences how rapidly performance improvements can be achieved. Flowcharts are the most extensively examined type of technical diagram, which has led to more advanced, fine-tuned, and higher-accuracy extraction techniques, along with publicly accessible benchmark datasets. Circuit diagrams have also received considerable research focus. However, progress in this area remains constrained by the reliance on small, domain-specific, custom-built datasets. In contrast, timing diagrams, block diagrams, and UML diagrams have received relatively limited attention, leading to slower methodological advancements and lower overall extraction performance. A notable issue identified in the reviewed research is the scarcity of publicly available datasets that can be reliably used for testing and evaluating extraction methods. Most studies relied on custom datasets tailored to their specific needs, which may introduce bias and limit the generalizability of their results. The availability of a standard public dataset could enable more robust and applicable benchmarking, supporting fairness in comparing various image content analysis approaches. 5. Conclusions With the rapid proliferation of LLMs and agentic AI in our everyday lives comes a resurgence in the need for multimodal data access to inform queries and produce accurate responses. As a result, we see growing research activities in image content analysis and extraction for complex technical illustrations, such as flowcharts, block diagrams, UML diagrams, schematics, and timing diagrams—illustrations where a significant portion of the information is represented not just by text, but by the contained shapes, their styling, and their relationships. To cope with increasingly complex diagrams and the need to reduce extraction errors, the techniques utilized for extracting information from these technical illustrations must also evolve. The rise of AI-powered techniques resulted in a number of highly promising image content analysis techniques, achieving higher accuracy and richer contextual representations than ever before, across a variety of diagram types. Our survey found that the majority of recent research activities focused on flowcharts, while circuit schematics represent the second-most studied diagram type. In contrast, block diagrams, UML diagrams, and timing diagrams have received only limited interest, with only two studies dedicated to block diagrams and timing diagrams, and five studies dedicated to UML diagrams. While the advancements made so far are promising, significant work remains as future research avenues to optimize extraction methods for various diagram types, reduce extraction errors, and improve the cost and complexity of these algorithms. Reducing complexity, in particular, is crucial for accelerating information extraction in an era where an ever-increasing amount of multimodal information is being generated. Similarly, for applications where accuracy is paramount, further research is necessary to enhance information extraction in terms of both completeness and accuracy. This is vital for reducing hallucinations and downstream generative errors in agentic AI systems. Finally, we also observed that the significant lack of benchmark datasets severely impedes objective comparisons across the various techniques. While several research works utilize larger datasets with thousands of images, others use much smaller datasets, sometimes comprised of only a few dozen images. Thus, there is a strong need to establish and publicly release benchmark datasets that span the full range of technical diagram types, with sufficient representation in terms of complexity, color usage, multilingual aspects, diagram correctness, and other features. This survey aims to provide both a cross-section of research work related to information extraction from technical diagrams, as well as inspire researchers to pursue the various open research questions in this field, in an effort to advance the accuracy, fidelity, and efficiency of these techniques, because there are numerous applications that would be enabled by these capabilities.
